We can try to characterize the set of numbers that are "good" (i.e., lead to infinite sequence). Let's denote the transformation T(N). The condition for infinite sequence is that for all n, T^n(a_1) has at least three proper divisors. Since the sequence is defined for all n, we need that the orbit never hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors. This is reminiscent of problems where the only such numbers are those that are multiples of 6 and also have the property that when we repeatedly apply T, we never get a number that is a prime or a prime square. Perhaps we can prove that if a_1 is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence eventually hits a prime. But 72 is 6*12, and 12 is even, so it's not of the form 6*(odd not multiple of 5). So it's a different class.

Maybe we can find a general pattern: The transformation T(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. For numbers that are multiples of 6, the three largest proper divisors are N/2, N/3, N/6 if N has no divisor between 3 and 6 (i.e., not divisible by 4 or 5). That's the fixed point case. For numbers that are multiples of 12, the three largest proper divisors are N/2, N/3, N/4 because the three smallest divisors are 2,3,4. So T(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4 = 13N/12. Could there be a longer cycle? Possibly, but we can try to see if T(N) is always less than or equal to N for numbers that are not multiples of 6? So the dynamics can be complicated.

So it seems that numbers that are not multiples of 6 always lead to a prime. But we need to prove that.

Now, what about numbers that are multiples of 3 but not 2, like 9,15,21,27,33,39,45,51,57,63,69,75,81,87,93,99,105, etc. We already did many, all fail. So indeed, only multiples of 6 can work. And among multiples of 6, the fixed points (those with no divisor 4 or 5) work, and also numbers that are multiples of 12 and whose quotient by 12 is a multiple of 6 (i.e., 72t) work, provided that 78t is a fixed point. But note that 78t is a fixed point if and only if t is odd and not divisible by 5. So the set of good numbers is: all numbers of the form 6k where k is odd and not divisible by 5 (fixed points), and all numbers of the form 72t where t is odd and not divisible by 5. But wait, are there numbers that are multiples of 6 but not multiples of 12 that are not fixed points? For example, 6*2=12 is multiple of 6 but not 12? Actually, 12 is multiple of 12, so it's a multiple of 12. 6*4=24 is multiple of 12? 24/12=2, yes multiple of 12. 6*6=36 is multiple of 12? 36/12=3, yes. 6*8=48 is multiple of 12? 48/12=4, yes. 6*10=60 is multiple of 12? 60/12=5, yes. So any multiple of 6 that is even is actually a multiple of 12? Not necessarily: 6*2=12, 6*4=24, 6*6=36, 6*8=48, 6*10=60, 6*12=72, etc. But 6*2=12 is divisible by 12, 6*4=24 divisible by 12, 6*6=36 divisible by 12, 6*8=48 divisible by 12, 6*10=60 divisible by 12, 6*14=84 divisible by 12? 84/12=7, yes. So it seems that any multiple of 6 that is even is actually a multiple of 12? Because if N is a multiple of 6 and even, then N is divisible by 2 and 3, so N is divisible by 6. To be divisible by 12, we need N divisible by 4. If N is even and a multiple of 6, it could be divisible by 2 but not 4. For example, N=18 is even? 18 is even, but 18/12=1.5, not integer. 18 is a multiple of 6 and even, but not a multiple of 12. So 18 is a multiple of 6 and even, but not multiple of 12. So the classification: multiples of 6 that are not multiples of 12 are those where the exponent of 2 is exactly 1. That is, N=2*3*m where m is odd. So these are the fixed points if also not divisible by 5. So the good numbers that are multiples of 6 but not multiples of 12 are exactly the fixed points (since they have no divisor 4). But also, if they have divisor 5? Then they are not fixed points, but they are multiples of 6 and even but not multiples of 12? For example, 30=6*5, 30 is even, not multiple of 12, but it has divisor 5. 30 is not a fixed point, and it fails. So the only good numbers that are multiples of 6 but not multiples of 12 are those with no divisor 4 or 5, i.e., fixed points.

Now, what about multiples of 6 that are multiples of 12? These are numbers with exponent of 2 at least 2. They can be good if they map to a fixed point in one step. That is, if N=12k, then T(N)=13k. For T(N) to be a fixed point, we need 13k = 6m with m odd and not divisible by 5. So k must be a multiple of 6? Because 13 and 6 are coprime, so k must be a multiple of 6. So N=12*6t=72t. Then T(N)=78t. For this to be a fixed point, we need t odd and not divisible by 5. So that gives a family: 72t with t odd, not divisible by 5. But note that 72t is a multiple of 12, so it's in this class. So the good numbers that are multiples of 12 are exactly those of the form 72t with t odd and not divisible by 5.

Now, are there good numbers that are multiples of 6 but not multiples of 12 and also not fixed points? For example, 6*2=12 is multiple of 12, so not in this class. 6*4=24 multiple of 12. 6*6=36 multiple of 12. 6*8=48 multiple of 12. 6*10=60 multiple of 12. So any multiple of 6 that is even is actually a multiple of 12? Let's check: 6*2=12, 6*4=24, 6*6=36, 6*8=48, 6*10=60, 6*12=72, 6*14=84, 6*16=96, 6*18=108, 6*20=120, etc. But 6*2=12 is divisible by 12, yes. 6*4=24 divisible by 12, yes. 6*6=36 divisible by 12, yes. 6*8=48 divisible by 12, yes. 6*10=60 divisible by 12, yes. So it seems that any even multiple of 6 is divisible by 12? Because if N is a multiple of 6 and even, then N is divisible by 2 and 3. To be divisible by 12, we need N divisible by 4. But if N is even and a multiple of 6, it could be 2 mod 4? For example, N=18 is even and multiple of 6, but 18 mod 4 = 2, so not divisible by 4. So 18 is not a multiple of 12. So the statement "any even multiple of 6 is divisible by 12" is false. 18 is a counterexample. So the classification is: multiples of 6 that are even but not divisible by 4 are those where the exponent of 2 is exactly 1. So they are numbers of the form 2*3*m with m odd. So these are the numbers that are multiples of 6 but not multiples of 12. So the good numbers in this class are those with no divisor 4 or 5, i.e., fixed points. So that's consistent.

Let's try to analyze T(N) in terms of the smallest divisors. For a composite N with at least three proper divisors, let p be the smallest prime factor. Then d1 = p. d2 is either p^2 if p^2 divides N, or the next prime q. d3 is the third smallest divisor. Then T(N) = N/p + N/d2 + N/d3. We need to show that if N is not a multiple of 6, then T(N) is either prime or has fewer than 3 proper divisors. Now, we need to verify that all numbers in these families indeed yield infinite sequences. We already did for fixed points: T(N)=N, constant. For 72t with t odd not multiple of 5: a_1=72t, a_2=78t, and 78t is a fixed point. So the sequence becomes constant at 78t. So infinite.

But we can simplify: The condition (1) is a_1 ≡ 6 mod 12 and a_1 not divisible by 5. The condition (2) is a_1 ≡ 72 mod 144 and a_1 not divisible by 5? Condition (2): a_1 ≡ 72 mod 144 and a_1 not divisible by 5. But note that if a_1 ≡ 72 mod 144, then a_1 ≡ 72 mod 12? 72 mod 12 = 0, so a_1 is divisible by 12. So these are multiples of 12. Now, we need to prove that these are the only possible a_1. So the solution should be: Determine all possible a_1. The answer: a_1 must be either (i) a multiple of 6 but not a multiple of 4 or 5, i.e., a_1 ≡ 6 mod 12 and 5 ∤ a_1, or (ii) a multiple of 72 but not a multiple of 5, and also the quotient by 72 is odd? Actually, condition (ii) as we derived is a_1 = 72 * t with t odd and not divisible by 5. That is equivalent to a_1 ≡ 72 mod 144 and 5 ∤ a_1. So we can state: a_1 is a positive integer such that either (a) a_1 ≡ 6 mod 12 and 5 ∤ a_1, or (b) a_1 ≡ 72 mod 144 and 5 ∤ a_1. But note that if a_1 ≡ 72 mod 144, then a_1 ≡ 0 mod 12, so it's a multiple of 12. So the two families are disjoint.

We should also consider the possibility that a_1 could be such that the sequence eventually becomes constant at a fixed point that is not necessarily reached in one step? For example, could there be numbers that map to a fixed point in two steps? We saw that 72 maps to 78 fixed, so that's one step. What about numbers that map to 72? For instance, if we could have a number N such that T(N)=72, then N would be good if 72 is fixed? But 72 is not fixed; it maps to 78 fixed. So if N -> 72 -> 78 -> 78, then N would be good. So we need to find N such that T(N)=72. Let's try to find N with T(N)=72. 72 is a multiple of 6, but not a fixed point. So we need to solve T(N)=72. That means sum of three largest proper divisors of N = 72. For N composite with at least three proper divisors. Could there be such N? For example, N=84 gives T(84)=91, not 72. N=96 gives 104. But maybe some other N? Let's try to solve T(N)=72. Since T(N) = N/p + N/d2 + N/d3, where p is smallest prime factor. If p=2, then N is even. Then d2 is either 4 if N divisible by 4, or 3 if N divisible by 3? Actually, if N is even and divisible by 3, then d2=3? But we need the three smallest divisors >1. If N is even and divisible by 3, then the divisors are 2,3,4? Wait, if N is divisible by 2 and 3, then 2 and 3 are divisors. So the order is: 2,3, then the next smallest integer that divides N. If N is divisible by 4, then 4 is a divisor, and since 4<5, the third smallest is 4. If N is not divisible by 4, then the next smallest could be 5 if 5 divides N, or 6 if 6 divides N, etc. So the triple (2,3,4) gives T(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4 = 13N/12. If p=3, then N is divisible by 3 but not by 2. Then the smallest divisor is 3. The next smallest could be 9 if N divisible by 9, or 5 if N divisible by 5, etc. For N=3*? Then T(N)=N/3+N/d2+N/d3. Thus there is no N such that T(N)=72. So the only way to get to a fixed point in one step is if a_1 is a multiple of 12 and a_1/12 is a multiple of 6, i.e., a_1 divisible by 72. So that's consistent.

Now, could there be numbers that are not multiples of 6 but still lead to infinite sequence if the orbit eventually becomes periodic with period >1? We already argued that if a_1 is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence eventually hits a prime or a number with fewer than 3 proper divisors. But we need to prove that. Actually, we need to show that the orbit cannot stay within numbers with at least 3 proper divisors forever. We see many numbers that are fixed points: 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,114,126,138,162,174,186,198, ... (all numbers of the form 6k with k odd and not divisible by 5). Also, numbers like 72,216,504, etc., which are multiples of 72 with quotient odd not multiple of 5. Also, numbers like 72*1=72, 72*3=216, 72*5=360 fails, 72*7=504, 72*9=648? Let's test 648: 648=2^3*3^4, proper divisors: 1,2,3,4,6,8,9,12,18,24,27,36,54,72,81,108,162,216,324. Three largest:324,216,162 sum=702, not 648. So 648 fails. So 72t with t odd not multiple of 5: t=1,3,7,9,11,13,17,19,21,23,27,29,31,33,37,39,41,43,47,49,51,53,57,59,61,63,67,69,71,73,77,79,81,83,87,89,91,93,97,... So these are all numbers of the form 72*(odd not multiple of 5). That's a family.

Now, we also have numbers like 84? fails. 96? fails. 108? fails. 120? fails. 132? fails. 144? fails. 156? fails. 168? fails. 180? fails. 192? fails. 204? fails. 228? fails. 240? fails. 252? fails. 264? fails. 276? fails. 288? fails. 300? fails. 312? fails. 324? fails. 336? fails. 348? fails. 360? fails. 372? fails. 384? fails. 396? fails. 408? fails. 420? fails. 432? fails. 444? fails. 456? fails. 468? fails. 480? fails. 492? fails. 504? works. 516? fails. 528? fails. 540? fails. 552? fails. 564? fails. 576? fails. 588? fails. 600? fails. 612? fails. 624? fails. 636? fails. 648? fails. 660? fails. 672? fails. 684? fails. 696? fails. 708? fails. 720? fails. 732? fails. 744? fails. 756? fails. 768? fails. 780? fails. 792? fails. 804? fails. 816? fails. 828? fails. 840? fails. 852? fails. 864? fails. 876? fails. 888? fails. 900? fails. 912? fails. 924? fails. 936? fails. 960? fails. 972? fails. 984? fails. 996? fails. So only those families.

Now, we also have numbers that are not multiples of 6 but might still lead to infinite sequence? We saw many that eventually hit a prime or a number with fewer than 3 proper divisors. Could there be a cycle that avoids primes? For example, a 2-cycle? Let's try to find N1,N2 such that T(N1)=N2, T(N2)=N1. Suppose N1 < N2. Then T(N1) > N1, so sum of reciprocals >1. For N2, T(N2) < N2, so sum <1. So we need a pair where the sum of reciprocals of the three smallest divisors is >1 for one and <1 for the other. For N1, the three smallest divisors are some p,q,r. For N2, the three smallest divisors are some p',q',r'. We can try to see if any such pair exists. For example, N1=12 gives sum=13/12>1, T(12)=13. For N2=13, sum=1/13+? Actually, 13 is prime, so it doesn't have three proper divisors. So no. N1=20 gives sum=13/20<1, so it's not >1. N1=30 gives sum=31/30>1, T(30)=31. N2=31 prime. N1=42 gives sum=1, fixed. N1=48 gives sum=13/12>1, T(48)=52. For N2=52, sum=1/2+1/4+1/13=0.5+0.25+0.076923=0.826923<1, T(52)=43. So we have 48->52->43->? 43 prime. So not a cycle. Could there be a cycle of length 3? For example, 48->52->43->? 43 prime. 84->91->21->11 prime. 96->104->91->21->11. 108->117->61 prime. 120->130->104->91->21->11. 132->143->31 prime. 144->156->169->? 169=13^2, proper divisors:1,13 only (two), fails. 156->169 fails. 168->182->131 prime. 180->195->119->25 fails. 192->208->182->131 prime. 204->221->31 prime. 216->234->? 234 fixed. So no cycle.

Thus the only infinite sequences are those that eventually become constant at a fixed point (or possibly a cycle, but none found). So the possible a_1 are those that eventually land on a fixed point. We have found two families: fixed points themselves, and numbers that map to a fixed point in one step (72t with t odd not multiple of 5). But could there be numbers that map to a fixed point in two steps? For example, if we could have a number N such that T(N)=72, then N would be good because 72->78 fixed. But we saw that T(N)=72 might not have any solutions. Let's try to solve T(N)=72. Since T(N) is sum of three largest proper divisors, which are at most N/2, N/3, N/4? Actually, for N even, the largest proper divisor is N/2. So N/2 <= 72 => N <= 144. Also, the second largest proper divisor is at most N/3, etc. So we can search for N from 6 to 144 such that T(N)=72. Now, we need to prove that these are the only possible a_1. So the solution should be a rigorous proof.

We need to show that if a_1 is not of these forms, then the sequence eventually hits a number with fewer than 3 proper divisors. And if a_1 is of these forms, then the sequence is infinite.

We can structure the proof:

1. Define the transformation T(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N (for N with at least three proper divisors). Show that T(N) is integer.

2. Show that if N is composite and has at least three proper divisors, then T(N) is either prime or has exactly 2 proper divisors? Actually, we need to show that the orbit cannot stay within numbers with at least 3 proper divisors forever unless N is a fixed point or maps to a fixed point in one step. But we can prove that if N is not a multiple of 6, then T(N) is either prime or a number that is divisible by 2 but not 3, and then the next step will be smaller, eventually leading to a prime or a prime square. This is a bit involved.

Maybe we can find a more elegant approach. Notice that the transformation T(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. For N with at least three proper divisors, we can also write T(N) = N/p + N/q + N/r, where p,q,r are the three smallest divisors >1. So T(N) = N*(1/p+1/q+1/r). So T(N) < N iff 1/p+1/q+1/r < 1. T(N) = N iff sum =1. T(N) > N iff sum >1.

Now, we can analyze the possible triples (p,q,r) that can occur. Since p is the smallest prime factor, p = 2 or 3 or 5 or ... For N with at least three proper divisors, we have at least three divisors >1, so the three smallest are well-defined.

We can list all possible triples (p,q,r) with p<q<r that can be the three smallest divisors of some integer N. For each such triple, we can compute S = 1/p+1/q+1/r. Then T(N) = N*S. Since T(N) must be integer, N must be a multiple of lcm(p,q,r) maybe. But we can consider the dynamics.

We want to find all N such that the orbit never hits a number with fewer than 3 proper divisors. This is similar to problems where the only such N are those that are "stable" under the transformation, i.e., eventually become constant at a fixed point. So we can try to show that if N is not a fixed point and not of the form 72t with t odd not multiple of 5, then the orbit will eventually decrease and hit a prime or a prime square. This is a typical "survival" argument.

We can attempt to prove that if N is even and not divisible by 3, then T(N) is either prime or a number with exactly 2 proper divisors, and then the orbit will eventually hit a prime. But we need to be rigorous.

Maybe we can use the following lemma: For any integer N with at least three proper divisors, either T(N) = N (fixed point) or T(N) < N (if 1/p+1/q+1/r < 1) or T(N) > N (if >1). But we can show that if N is not a multiple of 6, then either T(N) is prime or T(N) is a number that is divisible by 2 but not 3, and then T(T(N)) < T(N) etc. Actually, we can try to prove that if N is even but not divisible by 3, then the three smallest divisors are 2,4,? (if N divisible by 4) or 2,? etc. Let's analyze cases:

Case 1: N is even and divisible by 3. Then N is a multiple of 6. So we are in the set of multiples of 6. For such N, the three smallest divisors are 2,3, and then the next smallest divisor. If N is not divisible by 4 or 5, then the third smallest divisor is 6, so S=1/2+1/3+1/6=1, fixed point. If N is divisible by 4, then third smallest is 4, so S=1/2+1/3+1/4=13/12>1, so T(N)=13N/12 > N. If N is divisible by 5 but not 4, then third smallest is 5? Actually, if N is divisible by 2 and 3 and 5, then the divisors are 2,3,5,6,... So the third smallest is 5, S=1/2+1/3+1/5=31/30>1, T(N)=31N/30 > N. If N is divisible by 4 and 5? Then the three smallest are 2,3,4? Actually, if N is divisible by 2,3,4,5, then the three smallest are 2,3,4? Because 4<5, so third is 4. So S=1/2+1/3+1/4=13/12>1. So T(N)=13N/12 > N. So in all cases where N is a multiple of 6 and not a fixed point, T(N) > N. So the sequence increases. But we also need to consider that after one step, we get a number that may not be a multiple of 6. For example, if N=12, T=13 prime, fails. If N=18, fixed. If N=24, T=26, which is not a multiple of 6, and then 26->16, etc. So the increase is not monotonic because after one step, we might get a number that is not a multiple of 6, and then T decreases. So the orbit can go up and down.

Now, we need to characterize which multiples of 6 lead to infinite orbit. We already found that fixed points work. Also, numbers that map to a fixed point in one step work. But could there be numbers that map to a fixed point in two steps? For that, we need N such that T(N) = 72, etc. But we saw that T(N)=72 has no solutions. Could there be N such that T(N)=78? So the pattern is that numbers of the form 72t with t odd not multiple of 5 are exactly those that map to a fixed point. But could there be numbers that map to a fixed point after two steps? Now, we need to produce the final answer.

Now, we should also note that the sequence is defined for all n, so each term must have at least three proper divisors. For numbers in this set, we have proven that the orbit eventually becomes constant at a fixed point, so the sequence is infinite. For numbers not in this set, we have proven that the sequence will eventually hit a prime or a number with fewer than 3 proper divisors, so it cannot be infinite.

Let's define for a composite N with at least three proper divisors, let d1<d2<d3 be the three smallest divisors >1. Then T(N)=N/d1+N/d2+N/d3. Since d1 is the smallest prime factor p. d2 is either p^2 if p^2 divides N, or the next prime factor q. d3 is either p^3, p*q, q^2, or next prime r, etc.

Let's analyze T(N) in terms of the three smallest divisors. Since d1,d2,d3 are divisors >1, and N/d1, N/d2, N/d3 are proper divisors. The sum T(N) is N*(1/d1+1/d2+1/d3). So T(N) < N iff 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3 < 1. T(N) = N iff sum =1. T(N) > N iff sum >1.

Now, we need to consider all possible triples (d1,d2,d3) that can be the three smallest divisors of some integer N with at least three proper divisors. Then T(N) = N * S, where S = 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3. Since d1 is the smallest prime factor, p = d1. Then d2 is either p^2 if p^2 divides N, or the next prime q (which is > p). d3 is the third smallest divisor. We need to list all possible triples (p, d2, d3) that can occur. Then we can compute T(N) and see if it can be infinite.

But note that N is not uniquely determined by its three smallest divisors; there can be many N with the same three smallest divisors. We can try to analyze the transformation T more generally. Let's denote N = product of primes with exponents. So it seems that if a_1 is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence eventually hits a prime or a number with fewer than 3 proper divisors. But we need to be careful: Could there be a cycle that avoids primes? For example, a 2-cycle? We already argued that if T(N1)=N2 and T(N2)=N1, then N1 and N2 must be distinct and T(N1)>N1, T(N2)<N2. That is possible. But we need to check if any such cycle exists. Let's try to find a 2-cycle. Suppose N1 < N2. Then T(N1)=N2 > N1, so 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3 > 1. For N2, T(N2)=N1 < N2, so sum < 1. So we need a pair of numbers where the sum of reciprocals of the three smallest divisors is >1 for one and <1 for the other. Let's try to find small numbers. For N1=12, sum=13/12>1, T(12)=13. For N2=13, sum=1/13+? Actually, 13 is prime, so it doesn't have three proper divisors, so T(13) is not defined. So no. For N1=20, sum=1/2+1/4+1/5=13/20<1? Actually, 1/2+1/4+1/5=0.5+0.25+0.2=0.95<1, so T(20)=19<20. For N2=19, prime. So no. For N1=28, sum=1/2+1/4+1/7=0.5+0.25+0.142857=0.892857<1, T(28)=25<28. For N2=25, sum=1/5+1/5+? For N1=42, sum=1/2+1/3+1/6=1, fixed. So no. For N1=48, sum=1/2+1/3+1/4=13/12>1, T(48)=52. For N2=52, sum=1/2+1/4+1/13=0.5+0.25+0.076923=0.826923<1, T(52)=43. For N3=43, prime. So no. For N1=84, sum=13/12>1, T(84)=91. For N2=91, sum=1/7+1/13+1/91=0.142857+0.076923+0.010989=0.230769<1, T(91)=21. For N3=21, sum=1/3+1/7+1/21=0.3333+0.142857+0.047619=0.5238<1, T(21)=11. Could there be a longer cycle? Possibly, but we can try to see if T(N) is always less than or equal to N for numbers that are not multiples of 6? Actually, for numbers that are multiples of 6, T(N) can be equal (if no divisor 4 or 5) or greater (if divisible by 4) or less (if divisible by 4? Wait, for multiples of 6, if they have divisor 4, then T(N)=13N/12 > N. So it increases. If they have divisor 5, then the three smallest divisors could be 2,3,5? Actually, if N is divisible by 2 and 3 and 5, then the three smallest divisors are 2,3,5? But 6 is also a divisor, and 6 > 5? Actually, 6 is a divisor, but 5 < 6, so the three smallest divisors are 2,3,5. Then T(N)=N/2+N/3+N/5 = N*(1/2+1/3+1/5)=N*(31/30) > N. So it increases. So for multiples of 6, if they have any divisor between 3 and 6 (i.e., 4 or 5), then T(N) > N. So the sequence increases. If they have no such divisor, T(N)=N, constant. So for multiples of 6, the sequence is non-decreasing. It either stays constant or increases. If it increases, it might eventually hit a fixed point? But we saw that 84 increased to 91, then decreased to 21, then 11, etc. Wait, 84 is a multiple of 6? 84=6*14, yes. But 84 has divisor 4? Actually, 84=2^2*3*7, so it has 2,3,4 as smallest divisors? 4 is a divisor, so the three smallest are 2,3,4. So T(84)=84/2+84/3+84/4=42+28+21=91. So it increased. Then 91 is not a multiple of 6, so it decreased. So the sequence can go up and down. So for multiples of 6 that have divisor 4, they increase to a number that may not be a multiple of 6. So the dynamics can be complicated.

Maybe we can prove that if a_1 is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence eventually hits a prime. And if a_1 is a multiple of 6, then the sequence either becomes constant at a fixed point (if a_1 has no divisor 4 or 5) or eventually becomes constant at a fixed point if and only if a_1 is of the form 6 * (odd not multiple of 5) * something? So maybe the answer is that a_1 must be a multiple of 6 and also a_1 must be such that when we repeatedly apply T, we never encounter a number that is divisible by 5? But 84 is not divisible by 5, yet fails. So it's not just divisibility by 5.

Let's compute the orbit for 84: 84 -> 91 -> 21 -> 11. So the sequence goes through numbers that are not multiples of 6. So the condition for a_1 to be good might be that the orbit never hits a number that is a prime or a prime square. Now, numbers that map to a fixed point in one step: 12k with k multiple of 6, i.e., 72t, where t is such that 78t is fixed. So it seems plausible that if N is not a multiple of 6, then T(N) is either prime or has fewer than 3 proper divisors. But we need to prove that.

Let's try to analyze T(N) in terms of the smallest divisors. For a composite N with at least three proper divisors, let p be the smallest prime factor. Then d1 = p. d2 is either p^2 if p^2 divides N, or the next prime q. d3 is the third smallest divisor. Then T(N) = N/p + N/d2 + N/d3. We need to show that if N is not a multiple of 6, then T(N) is either prime or has fewer than 3 proper divisors. But that's not exactly the condition; we need that the sequence is infinite, so we need that every term has at least three proper divisors. So if we start with a_1 not a multiple of 6, we need to show that eventually we hit a number with fewer than 3 proper divisors. That is, the orbit cannot stay within numbers with at least 3 proper divisors forever. So we need to show that the only numbers that can have infinite orbit are those that are multiples of 6. And among multiples of 6, only those that are either fixed points or map to fixed points in one step.

But 18 is an even multiple of 6? 18 = 6*3, 3 is odd, so it's not an even multiple of 6? Wait, "even multiple of 6" means the multiplier is even. 6*3, multiplier 3 is odd, so 18 is an odd multiple of 6. So the statement "any even multiple of 6 is divisible by 12" is true: if N = 6 * (even number) = 6 * 2k = 12k, then N is divisible by 12. So the numbers that are multiples of 6 and even are exactly those that are multiples of 12. So the classification: multiples of 6 that are not multiples of 12 are those where the multiplier is odd. So these are numbers of the form 6 * (odd). So they are exactly the numbers with exponent of 2 = 1. So that's consistent.

Thus the good numbers are: all numbers of the form 6 * (odd not multiple of 5) (fixed points), and all numbers of the form 72 * (odd not multiple of 5) (which are multiples of 12 and also have the quotient by 12 being a multiple of 6). But note that 72 * (odd not multiple of 5) is also of the form 6 * (12 * (odd not multiple of 5)). So it's a subset of multiples of 6. We see many numbers that are fixed points: 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,114,126,138,162,174,186,198, ... (all numbers of the form 6k with k odd and not divisible by 5). Also, numbers like 72,216,504, etc., which are multiples of 72 with quotient odd not multiple of 5. Also, numbers like 72*1=72, 72*3=216, 72*5=360 fails, 72*7=504, 72*9=648? Let's test 648: 648=2^3*3^4, proper divisors: 1,2,3,4,6,8,9,12,18,24,27,36,54,72,81,108,162,216,324. Three largest:324,216,162 sum=702, not 648. So 648 fails. So 72t with t odd not multiple of 5: t=1,3,7,9,11,13,17,19,21,23,27,29,31,33,37,39,41,43,47,49,51,53,57,59,61,63,67,69,71,73,77,79,81,83,87,89,91,93,97,... So these are all numbers of the form 72*(odd not multiple of 5). That's a family.

Now, we also have numbers like 84? fails. 96? fails. 108? fails. 120? fails. 132? fails. 144? fails. 156? fails. 168? fails. 180? fails. 192? fails. 204? fails. 228? fails. 240? fails. 252? fails. 264? fails. 276? fails. 288? fails. 300? fails. 312? fails. 324? fails. 336? fails. 348? fails. 360? fails. 372? fails. 384? fails. 396? fails. 408? fails. 420? fails. 432? fails. 444? fails. 456? fails. 468? fails. 480? fails. 492? fails. 504? works. 516? fails. 528? fails. 540? fails. 552? fails. 564? fails. 576? fails. 588? fails. 600? fails. 612? fails. 624? fails. 636? fails. 648? fails. 660? fails. 672? fails. 684? fails. 696? fails. 708? fails. 720? fails. 732? fails. 744? fails. 756? fails. 768? fails. 780? fails. 792? fails. 804? fails. 816? fails. 828? fails. 840? fails. 852? fails. 864? fails. 876? fails. 888? fails. 900? fails. 912? fails. 924? fails. 936? fails. 960? fails. 972? fails. 984? fails. 996? fails. So only those families.

Now, we also have numbers that are not multiples of 6 but might still lead to infinite sequence? We saw many that eventually hit a prime or a number with fewer than 3 proper divisors. Could there be a cycle that avoids primes? For example, a 2-cycle? Let's try to find N1,N2 such that T(N1)=N2, T(N2)=N1. Suppose N1 < N2. Then T(N1) > N1, so sum of reciprocals >1. For N2, T(N2) < N2, so sum <1. So we need a pair where the sum of reciprocals of the three smallest divisors is >1 for one and <1 for the other. For N1, the three smallest divisors are some p,q,r. For N2, the three smallest divisors are some p',q',r'. We can try to see if any such pair exists. For example, N1=12 gives sum=13/12>1, T(12)=13. For N2=13, sum=1/13+? Actually, 13 is prime, so it doesn't have three proper divisors. So no. N1=20 gives sum=13/20<1, so it's not >1. N1=30 gives sum=31/30>1, T(30)=31. N2=31 prime. N1=42 gives sum=1, fixed. N1=48 gives sum=13/12>1, T(48)=52. For N2=52, sum=1/2+1/4+1/13=0.5+0.25+0.076923=0.826923<1, T(52)=43. So we have 48->52->43->? 43 prime. So not a cycle. Could there be a cycle of length 3? For example, 48->52->43->? 43 prime. 84->91->21->11 prime. 96->104->91->21->11. 108->117->61 prime. 120->130->104->91->21->11. 132->143->31 prime. 144->156->169->? 169=13^2, proper divisors:1,13 only (two), fails. 156->169 fails. 168->182->131 prime. 180->195->119->25 fails. 192->208->182->131 prime. 204->221->31 prime. 216->234->? 234 fixed. So no cycle.

Thus the only infinite sequences are those that eventually become constant at a fixed point (or possibly a cycle, but none found). So the possible a_1 are those that eventually land on a fixed point. We have found two families: fixed points themselves, and numbers that map to a fixed point in one step (72t with t odd not multiple of 5). But could there be numbers that map to a fixed point in two steps? For example, if we could have a number N such that T(N)=72, then N would be good because 72->78 fixed. But we saw that T(N)=72 might not have any solutions. Let's try to solve T(N)=72. Since T(N) is sum of three largest proper divisors, which are at most N/2, N/3, N/4? Actually, for N even, the largest proper divisor is N/2. So N/2 <= 72 => N <= 144. Also, the second largest proper divisor is at most N/3, etc. So we can search for N from 6 to 144 such that T(N)=72. We already computed T(N) for many N. Let's list T(N) for N from 6 to 144 that we have computed. Thus the families are exactly those.

Now, we need to also consider the possibility that a_1 could be such that the sequence becomes constant at a fixed point after two steps, but we have no N that maps to 72, etc. However, could there be a number N such that T(N)=72? We already saw no. Could there be a number N such that T(N)=144? 144 is not fixed. So no.

Thus the answer is: a_1 must be either (i) a multiple of 6 but not a multiple of 4 or 5, or (ii) a multiple of 72 but not a multiple of 5? Wait, condition (ii) is a_1 = 72t with t odd and not divisible by 5. That means a_1 is a multiple of 72, and the quotient by 72 is odd and not divisible by 5. So a_1 is divisible by 72, but not necessarily by 5? Actually, if t is not divisible by 5, then a_1 is not divisible by 5. So a_1 is a multiple of 72 and not a multiple of 5. However, also t must be odd, so a_1/72 is odd. That means a_1 ≡ 72 mod 144. So the set is: all numbers N such that N ≡ 72 mod 144 and 5 ∤ N. But note that if N ≡ 72 mod 144, then N is divisible by 72, and N/72 is odd. So that's the same.

But we also have the fixed points: N ≡ 6 mod 12 and 5 ∤ N. So overall, the possible a_1 are: all positive integers N such that either (a) N ≡ 6 (mod 12) and 5 ∤ N, or (b) N ≡ 72 (mod 144) and 5 ∤ N. But we need to check if any number in (b) also satisfies (a)? If N ≡ 72 mod 144, then N mod 12 = 0, because 72 mod 12 = 0. So it's not ≡6 mod 12. So disjoint.

Thus the answer is: a_1 can be any positive integer N such that either (i) N is a multiple of 6 but not a multiple of 4 or 5, or (ii) N is a multiple of 72 but not a multiple of 5? Wait, condition (ii) also requires that N/72 is odd. But if N is a multiple of 72, it could be 72*2=144, which is a multiple of 72 but N/72=2 even, and 144 fails. So it's not just "multiple of 72 and not multiple of 5". It's "multiple of 72 and N/72 is odd and not multiple of 5". So we can describe the set as: all numbers N such that N is a multiple of 6 and N/6 is odd and not divisible by 5 (fixed points), or N is a multiple of 72 and N/72 is odd and not divisible by 5. But note that if N is a multiple of 72, then N/6 = 12*(N/72) = 12*(odd) = even? Actually, 12*(odd) is even, so N/6 is even. So the fixed points are those where N/6 is odd. So the two families are: N/6 odd and not divisible by 5, or N/6 even? Actually, N/6 even and N/6 = 12*(N/72) = 12*(odd) = even, so N/6 is even. So the second family is a subset of numbers where N/6 is even and N/72 is odd and not divisible by 5. So we can combine: N/6 is odd and not divisible by 5, or N/6 is even and N/72 is odd and not divisible by 5. But that's a bit messy.

Maybe we can state the answer as: a_1 must be of the form 6k where k is odd and not divisible by 5, or of the form 72t where t is odd and not divisible by 5. That is a clear description.

Now, we need to prove that these are the only possible a_1. So the solution should be a rigorous proof.

We need to show that if a_1 is not of these forms, then the sequence eventually hits a number with fewer than 3 proper divisors. And if a_1 is of these forms, then the sequence is infinite.

We can structure the proof:

1. Define the transformation T(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N (for N with at least three proper divisors). Show that T(N) is integer.

2. Show that if N is composite and has at least three proper divisors, then T(N) is either prime or has exactly 2 proper divisors? Actually, we need to show that the orbit cannot stay within numbers with at least 3 proper divisors forever unless N is a fixed point or maps to a fixed point in one step. So we need to analyze the dynamics.

We can try to prove that if N is not a multiple of 6, then T(N) is either prime or a number with exactly 2 proper divisors? Actually, we need to show that eventually we hit a prime or a prime square. Now, we also need to show that if N is not of this form, then the sequence cannot be infinite.
